	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Area 51</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the U.S. Air Force facility in Nevada. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Area_51_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Area 51 (disambiguation)">Area 51 (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<p><b>Area 51</b> is the common name of a highly classified <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">United States Air Force</a> (USAF) facility located within the <a href="/wiki/Nevada_Test_and_Training_Range" title="Nevada Test and Training Range">Nevada Test and Training Range</a>. The facility is officially called <b>Homey Airport</b> (<b>KXTA</b>) or <b>Groom Lake</b>, named after <a href="/wiki/Groom_Lake_(salt_flat)" title="Groom Lake (salt flat)">the salt flat</a> situated next to its airfield. Details of the facility's operations are not publicly known, but the USAF says that it is an open training range,<sup id="cite_ref-McAndrewsWaPo_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McAndrewsWaPo-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> and it most likely supports the development and testing of experimental aircraft and weapons systems.<sup id="cite_ref-rich_groom_1977_p57_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rich_groom_1977_p57-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McAndrewsWaPo_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McAndrewsWaPo-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The USAF acquired the site in 1955, primarily for flight testing the <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_U-2" title="Lockheed U-2">Lockheed U-2</a> aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The intense secrecy surrounding the base has made it the frequent subject of conspiracy theories and a central component of <a href="/wiki/Unidentified_flying_object" title="Unidentified flying object">unidentified flying object</a> (UFO) folklore.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJacobsen2012_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJacobsen2012-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lacitis20100327_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lacitis20100327-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The base has never been declared a secret base, but all research and occurrences in Area 51 are <a href="/wiki/Top_Secret/Sensitive_Compartmented_Information" class="mw-redirect" title="Top Secret/Sensitive Compartmented Information">Top Secret/Sensitive Compartmented Information</a> (TS/SCI).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJacobsen2012_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJacobsen2012-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The CIA publicly acknowledged the existence of the base for the first time on June 25, 2013, following a <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_Information_Act_(United_States)" title="Freedom of Information Act (United States)">Freedom of Information Act</a> (FOIA) request filed in 2005, and they declassified documents detailing the history and purpose of Area 51.<sup id="cite_ref-cia1992_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia1992-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Area 51 is located in the southern portion of <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a> in the western United States, 83 miles (134&#160;km) north-northwest of <a href="/wiki/Las_Vegas" title="Las Vegas">Las Vegas</a>. The surrounding area is a popular tourist destination, including the small town of <a href="/wiki/Rachel,_Nevada" title="Rachel, Nevada">Rachel</a> on the "<a href="/wiki/Nevada_State_Route_375" title="Nevada State Route 375">Extraterrestrial Highway</a>".
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Civil_aviation_identification"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Civil aviation identification</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#Security"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Security</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#1974_Skylab_photography"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">1974 Skylab photography</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#UFO_and_other_conspiracy_theories"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">UFO and other conspiracy theories</span></a>
<ul>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Facebook_event_to_storm_Area_51"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Facebook event to storm Area 51</span></a></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>

</ul>
</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Area_51">Area 51</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Area51_Tikaboo_Peak_07.2008.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Area51_Tikaboo_Peak_07.2008.jpg/220px-Area51_Tikaboo_Peak_07.2008.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="125" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Area51_Tikaboo_Peak_07.2008.jpg/330px-Area51_Tikaboo_Peak_07.2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Area51_Tikaboo_Peak_07.2008.jpg/440px-Area51_Tikaboo_Peak_07.2008.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3869" data-file-height="2205" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Area51_Tikaboo_Peak_07.2008.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Area 51 viewed from distant <a href="/wiki/Tikaboo_Peak" title="Tikaboo Peak">Tikaboo Peak</a></div></div></div>
<p>The original rectangular base of 6 by 10 miles (9.7 by 16.1&#160;km) is now part of the so-called "Groom box", a rectangular area measuring 23 by 25 miles (37 by 40&#160;km), of restricted airspace. The area is connected to the internal <a href="/wiki/Nevada_Test_Site" title="Nevada Test Site">Nevada Test Site</a> (NTS) road network, with paved roads leading south to <a href="/wiki/Mercury,_Nevada" title="Mercury, Nevada">Mercury</a> and west to <a href="/wiki/Yucca_Flat" title="Yucca Flat">Yucca Flat</a>. Leading northeast from the lake, the wide and well-maintained Groom Lake Road runs through a pass in the Jumbled Hills. The road formerly led to mines in the Groom basin, but has been improved since their closure. Its winding course runs past a security checkpoint, but the restricted area around the base extends farther east. After leaving the restricted area, Groom Lake Road descends eastward to the floor of the <a href="/wiki/Tikaboo_Valley" title="Tikaboo Valley">Tikaboo Valley</a>, passing the dirt-road entrances to several small ranches, before converging with <a href="/wiki/Nevada_State_Route_375" title="Nevada State Route 375">State Route 375, the "Extraterrestrial Highway"</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> south of <a href="/wiki/Rachel,_Nevada" title="Rachel, Nevada">Rachel</a>.
</p><p>Area 51 shares a border with the <a href="/wiki/Yucca_Flat" title="Yucca Flat">Yucca Flat</a> region of the Nevada Test Site, the location of 739 of the 928 nuclear tests conducted by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">United States Department of Energy</a> at NTS.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ndepmap_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ndepmap-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Yucca_Mountain_nuclear_waste_repository" title="Yucca Mountain nuclear waste repository">Yucca Mountain nuclear waste repository</a> is 44 miles (71&#160;km) southwest of Groom Lake.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Groom_Lake">Groom Lake</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Groom_Lake_(salt_flat)" title="Groom Lake (salt flat)">Groom Lake</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Salt_pan_(geology)" title="Salt pan (geology)">salt flat</a><sup id="cite_ref-LeibyWaPo_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LeibyWaPo-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a> used for runways of the Nellis Bombing Range Test Site airport (KXTA) on the north of the Area 51 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">USAF</a> military installation. The lake at 4,409&#160;ft (1,344&#160;m)<sup id="cite_ref-GNIS_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GNIS-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> elevation is approximately 3.7 miles (6.0&#160;km) from north to south and 3 miles (4.8&#160;km) from east to west at its widest point. Located within the namesake Groom Lake Valley portion of the <a href="/wiki/Tonopah_Basin" class="mw-redirect" title="Tonopah Basin">Tonopah Basin</a>, the lake is 25&#160;mi (40&#160;km) south of <a href="/wiki/Rachel,_Nevada" title="Rachel, Nevada">Rachel, Nevada</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NevadaTestRange_4808A.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/NevadaTestRange_4808A.png/220px-NevadaTestRange_4808A.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/NevadaTestRange_4808A.png/330px-NevadaTestRange_4808A.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/NevadaTestRange_4808A.png/440px-NevadaTestRange_4808A.png 2x" data-file-width="1205" data-file-height="721" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NevadaTestRange_4808A.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nevada Test Range topographic chart centered on Groom Lake</div></div></div>
<p>The origin of the name "Area 51" is unclear. It is believed to be from an Atomic Energy Commission (AEC) numbering grid, although Area 51 is not part of this system; it is adjacent to Area 15. Another explanation is that 51 was used because it was unlikely that the AEC would use the number.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">Central Intelligence Agency</a> (CIA), the correct names for the facility are Homey Airport (KXTA) and Groom Lake,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FASOverhead_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FASOverhead-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> though the name <i>Area 51</i> was used in a CIA document from the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_oxcart_kadena_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia_oxcart_kadena-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The facility has also been referred to as <i>Dreamland</i> and <i>Paradise Ranch</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-redflag_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-redflag-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rich_groom_1977_p56_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rich_groom_1977_p56-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> among other nicknames. The USAF public relations has referred to the facility as "an operating location near Groom Dry Lake". The <a href="/wiki/Special_use_airspace" title="Special use airspace">special use airspace</a> around the field is referred to as Restricted Area 4808 North (R-4808N).<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lead and silver were discovered in the southern part of the Groom Range in 1864,<sup id="cite_ref-nevada-bureau_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevada-bureau-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> and the English company <i>Groome Lead Mines Limited</i> financed the Conception Mines in the 1870s, giving the district its name (nearby mines included Maria, Willow, and White Lake).<sup id="cite_ref-unr-guide-groom-mining_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unr-guide-groom-mining-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> J. B. Osborne and partners acquired the interests in Groom in 1876, and his son acquired the interests in the 1890s.<sup id="cite_ref-unr-guide-groom-mining_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unr-guide-groom-mining-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Mining continued until 1918, then resumed after World War II until the early 1950s.<sup id="cite_ref-unr-guide-groom-mining_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unr-guide-groom-mining-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The airfield on the Groom Lake site began service in 1942 as Indian Springs Air Force Auxiliary Field<sup id="cite_ref-mueller_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mueller-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> and consisted of two unpaved 5,000-foot runways at <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Area_51&amp;params=37_16_35_N_115_45_20_W_"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">37°16′35″N</span> <span class="longitude">115°45′20″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">&#xfeff; / &#xfeff;</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">37.27639°N 115.75556°W</span><span style="display:none">&#xfeff; / <span class="geo">37.27639; -115.75556</span></span></span></a></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="U-2_program">U-2 program</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_U-2" title="Lockheed U-2">Lockheed U-2</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Area51WatertownNE.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Area51WatertownNE.jpg/220px-Area51WatertownNE.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Area51WatertownNE.jpg/330px-Area51WatertownNE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Area51WatertownNE.jpg/440px-Area51WatertownNE.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="475" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Area51WatertownNE.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"The Ranch" with U-2 flight line</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">Central Intelligence Agency</a> (CIA) established the Groom Lake test facility in April 1955 for Project AQUATONE, the development of the <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_U-2" title="Lockheed U-2">Lockheed U-2</a> strategic reconnaissance aircraft. Project director <a href="/wiki/Richard_M._Bissell_Jr." title="Richard M. Bissell Jr.">Richard M. Bissell Jr.</a> understood that the flight test and pilot training programs could not be conducted at <a href="/wiki/Edwards_Air_Force_Base" title="Edwards Air Force Base">Edwards Air Force Base</a> or Lockheed's Palmdale facility, given the extreme secrecy surrounding the project. He conducted a search for a suitable testing site for the U-2 under the same extreme security as the rest of the project.<sup id="cite_ref-Peebles_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peebles-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> He notified Lockheed, who sent an inspection team out to Groom Lake. According to Lockheed's U-2 designer <a href="/wiki/Kelly_Johnson_(engineer)" title="Kelly Johnson (engineer)">Kelly Johnson</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-Peebles_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peebles-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>We flew over it and within thirty seconds, you knew that was the place<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... it was right by a dry lake. Man alive, we looked at that lake, and we all looked at each other. It was another Edwards, so we wheeled around, landed on that lake, taxied up to one end of it. It was a perfect natural landing field<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... as smooth as a billiard table without anything being done to it.</p></blockquote>
<p>The lake bed made an ideal strip for testing aircraft, and the Emigrant Valley's mountain ranges and the NTS perimeter protected the site from visitors, about 100&#160;mi (160&#160;km) north of Las Vegas.<sup id="cite_ref-shadow_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shadow-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The CIA asked the AEC to acquire the land, designated "Area 51" on the map, and to add it to the Nevada Test Site.<sup id="cite_ref-cia1992_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia1992-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>56–57</span></sup>
</p><p>Johnson named the area "Paradise Ranch" to encourage workers to move to "the new facility in the middle of nowhere", as the CIA later described it, and the name became shortened to "the Ranch".<sup id="cite_ref-cia1992_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia1992-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>57</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Powers_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Powers-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> On 4<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>May 1955, a survey team arrived at Groom Lake and laid out a 5,000-foot (1,500&#160;m) north–south runway on the southwest corner of the lakebed and designated a site for a base support facility. The Ranch initially consisted of little more than a few shelters, workshops, and trailer homes in which to house its small team.<sup id="cite_ref-shadow_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shadow-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> In a little over three months, the base consisted of a single paved runway, three hangars, a control tower, and rudimentary accommodations for test personnel. The base's few amenities included a movie theater and volleyball court. There was also a mess hall, several wells, and fuel storage tanks. CIA, Air Force, and Lockheed personnel began arriving by July 1955. The Ranch received its first U-2 delivery on 24 July 1955 from Burbank on a <a href="/wiki/C-124_Globemaster_II" class="mw-redirect" title="C-124 Globemaster II">C-124 Globemaster II</a> cargo plane, accompanied by Lockheed technicians on a <a href="/wiki/Douglas_DC-3" title="Douglas DC-3">Douglas DC-3</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-shadow_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shadow-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Regular Military Air Transport Service flights were set up between Area 51 and Lockheed's offices in <a href="/wiki/Burbank,_California" title="Burbank, California">Burbank, California</a>. To preserve secrecy, personnel flew to Nevada on Monday mornings and returned to California on Friday evenings.<sup id="cite_ref-cia1992_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia1992-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>72</span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="OXCART_program">OXCART program</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For testing of a similar aircraft in December 1964, see <a href="/wiki/SR-71_Blackbird" class="mw-redirect" title="SR-71 Blackbird">SR-71 Blackbird</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Area_51_-_diagram.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Area_51_-_diagram.jpg/220px-Area_51_-_diagram.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Area_51_-_diagram.jpg/330px-Area_51_-_diagram.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Area_51_-_diagram.jpg/440px-Area_51_-_diagram.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1174" data-file-height="950" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Area_51_-_diagram.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1966 <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">Central Intelligence Agency</a> (CIA) diagram of Area 51, found in an untitled, declassified paper, showing the runway overrun for OXCART (<a href="/wiki/Lockheed_A-12" title="Lockheed A-12">Lockheed A-12</a>) and the turnaround areas <i>(CIA / CREST RDP90b00184r000100040001-4)</i></div></div></div>
<p>Project OXCART was established in August 1959 for "antiradar studies, aerodynamic structural tests, and engineering designs" and all later work on the <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_A-12" title="Lockheed A-12">Lockheed A-12</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> This included testing at Groom Lake, which had inadequate facilities consisting of buildings for only 150 people, a 5,000&#160;ft (1,500&#160;m) asphalt runway, and limited fuel, hangar, and shop space.<sup id="cite_ref-Peebles_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peebles-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Groom Lake had received the name "Area 51"<sup id="cite_ref-Peebles_24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peebles-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (September 2013)">verification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> when A-12 test facility construction began in September 1960, including a new 8,500&#160;ft (2,600&#160;m) runway to replace the existing runway.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Reynolds Electrical and Engineering Company (REECo) began construction of "Project 51" on 1<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>October 1960 with double-shift construction schedules. The contractor upgraded base facilities and built a new 10,000&#160;ft (3,000&#160;m) runway (14/32) diagonally across the southwest corner of the lakebed. They marked an <a href="/wiki/Archimedean_spiral" title="Archimedean spiral">Archimedean spiral</a> on the dry lake approximately two miles across so that an A-12 pilot approaching the end of the overrun could abort instead of plunging into the sagebrush. Area 51 pilots called it "The Hook". For crosswind landings, they marked two unpaved airstrips (runways 9/27 and 03/21) on the dry lakebed.<sup id="cite_ref-TheOxcartStory_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TheOxcartStory-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By August 1961, construction of the essential facilities was complete; three surplus Navy hangars were erected on the base's north side while hangar<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>7 was new construction. The original U-2 hangars were converted to maintenance and machine shops. Facilities in the main cantonment area included workshops and buildings for storage and administration, a commissary, control tower, fire station, and housing. The Navy also contributed more than 130 surplus Babbitt duplex housing units for long-term occupancy facilities. Older buildings were repaired, and additional facilities were constructed as necessary. A reservoir pond surrounded by trees served as a recreational area one mile north of the base. Other recreational facilities included a gymnasium, a movie theater, and a baseball diamond.<sup id="cite_ref-TheOxcartStory_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TheOxcartStory-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> A permanent aircraft fuel tank farm was constructed by early 1962 for the special <a href="/wiki/JP-7" title="JP-7">JP-7</a> fuel required by the A-12. Seven tanks were constructed, with a total capacity of 1,320,000 gallons.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A-12_Schalk_Flight,_1962.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/A-12_Schalk_Flight%2C_1962.jpg/220px-A-12_Schalk_Flight%2C_1962.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="247" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/A-12_Schalk_Flight%2C_1962.jpg/330px-A-12_Schalk_Flight%2C_1962.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/A-12_Schalk_Flight%2C_1962.jpg/440px-A-12_Schalk_Flight%2C_1962.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6000" data-file-height="6724" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A-12_Schalk_Flight,_1962.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An A-12 (60-6924) takes off from Groom Lake during one of the first test flights, piloted by Louis Schalk, 26 April 1962.</div></div></div>
<p>Security was enhanced for the arrival of OXCART and the small mine was closed in the Groom basin. In January 1962, the Federal Aviation Administration (FAA) expanded the restricted airspace in the vicinity of Groom Lake, and the lakebed became the center of a 600 square-mile addition to restricted area R-4808N.<sup id="cite_ref-TheOxcartStory_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TheOxcartStory-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The CIA facility received eight USAF <a href="/wiki/F-101_Voodoo" class="mw-redirect" title="F-101 Voodoo">F-101 Voodoos</a> for training, two <a href="/wiki/T-33_Shooting_Star" class="mw-redirect" title="T-33 Shooting Star">T-33 Shooting Star</a> trainers for proficiency flying, a <a href="/wiki/C-130_Hercules" class="mw-redirect" title="C-130 Hercules">C-130 Hercules</a> for cargo transport, a U-3A for administrative purposes, a helicopter for search and rescue, and a <a href="/wiki/Cessna_180" title="Cessna 180">Cessna 180</a> for liaison use, and Lockheed provided an <a href="/wiki/F-104_Starfighter" class="mw-redirect" title="F-104 Starfighter">F-104 Starfighter</a> for use as a chase plane.<sup id="cite_ref-TheOxcartStory_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TheOxcartStory-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first A-12 test aircraft was covertly trucked from Burbank on 26 February 1962 and arrived at Groom Lake on 28 February.<sup id="cite_ref-Timeline_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Timeline-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> It made its first flight 26 April 1962 when the base had over 1,000 personnel. Initially, all who were not connected with a test were herded into the mess hall before each takeoff. This was soon dropped, as it disrupted activities and was impractical with the large number of flights.<sup id="cite_ref-Peebles_24-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peebles-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The closed airspace above Groom Lake was within the <a href="/wiki/Nellis_Air_Force_Range" class="mw-redirect" title="Nellis Air Force Range">Nellis Air Force Range</a> airspace, and pilots saw the A-12 20 to 30 times.<sup id="cite_ref-Peebles_24-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peebles-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Groom was also the site of the first <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_D-21" title="Lockheed D-21">Lockheed D-21</a> drone test flight on 22 December 1964.<sup id="cite_ref-Timeline_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Timeline-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> By the end of 1963, nine A-12s were at Area 51, assigned to the CIA-operated "1129th Special Activities Squadron".<sup id="cite_ref-1129CIA_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1129CIA-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="D-21_Tagboard">D-21 Tagboard</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_D-21" title="Lockheed D-21">Lockheed D-21</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:M21Ship2-cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/M21Ship2-cropped.jpg/220px-M21Ship2-cropped.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/M21Ship2-cropped.jpg/330px-M21Ship2-cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/M21Ship2-cropped.jpg/440px-M21Ship2-cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="607" data-file-height="384" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:M21Ship2-cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The D-21 mounted on the back of the M-21. Note the intake cover on the drone, which was used on early flights.</div></div></div>
<p>Following the loss of <a href="/wiki/Gary_Powers" class="mw-redirect" title="Gary Powers">Gary Powers</a>' <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_U-2" title="Lockheed U-2">U-2</a> over the Soviet Union, there were several discussions about using the A-12 OXCART as an unpiloted drone aircraft. Although Kelly Johnson had come to support the idea of drone reconnaissance, he opposed the development of an A-12 drone, contending that the aircraft was too large and complex for such a conversion. However, the Air Force agreed to fund the study of a high-speed, high-altitude drone aircraft in October 1962. The Air Force interest seems to have moved the CIA to take action, the project designated "Q-12". By October 1963, the drone's design had been finalized. At the same time, the Q-12 underwent a name change. To separate it from the other A-12-based projects, it was renamed the "D-21". (The "12" was reversed to "21"). "Tagboard" was the project's code name.<sup id="cite_ref-Peebles_24-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peebles-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first D-21 was completed in the spring of 1964 by Lockheed. After four more months of checkouts and static tests, the aircraft was shipped to Groom Lake and reassembled. It was to be carried by a two-seat derivative of the A-12, designated the "M-21". When the D-21/M-21 reached the launch point, the first step would be to blow off the D-21's inlet and exhaust covers. With the D-21/M-21 at the correct speed and altitude, the LCO would start the ramjet and the other systems of the D-21. "With the D-21's systems activated and running, and the launch aircraft at the correct point, the M-21 would begin a slight pushover, the LCO would push a final button, and the D-21 would come off the pylon".<sup id="cite_ref-Peebles_24-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peebles-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Difficulties were addressed throughout 1964 and 1965 at Groom Lake with various technical issues. Captive flights showed unforeseen aerodynamic difficulties. By late January 1966, more than a year after the first captive flight, everything seemed ready. The first D-21 launch was made on 5<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>March 1966 with a successful flight, with the D-21 flying 120 miles with limited fuel. A second D-12 flight was successful in April 1966 with the drone flying 1,200 miles, reaching Mach 3.3 and 90,000 feet. An accident on 30 July 1966 with a fully fueled D-21, on a planned checkout flight suffered from an <a href="/wiki/Unstart" title="Unstart">unstart</a> of the drone after its separation, causing it to collide with the M-21 launch aircraft. The two crewmen ejected and landed in the ocean 150 miles offshore. One crew member was picked up by a helicopter, but the other, having survived the aircraft breakup and ejection, drowned when sea water entered his pressure suit. Kelly Johnson personally cancelled the entire program, having had serious doubts from the start of the feasibility. A number of D-21s had already been produced, and rather than scrapping the whole effort, Johnson again proposed to the Air Force that they be launched from a <a href="/wiki/Boeing_B-52_Stratofortress" title="Boeing B-52 Stratofortress">B-52H</a> bomber.<sup id="cite_ref-Peebles_24-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peebles-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By late summer of 1967, the modification work to both the D-21 (now designated D-21B) and the B-52Hs were complete. The test program could now resume. The test missions were flown out of Groom Lake, with the actual launches over the Pacific. The first D-21B to be flown was Article 501, the prototype. The first attempt was made on 28 September 1967, and ended in complete failure. As the B-52 was flying toward the launch point, the D-21B fell off the pylon. The B-52H gave a sharp lurch as the drone fell free. The booster fired and was "quite a sight from the ground". The failure was traced to a stripped nut on the forward right attachment point on the pylon. Several more tests were made, none of which met with success. However, the fact is that the resumptions of D-21 tests took place against a changing reconnaissance background. The A-12 had finally been allowed to deploy, and the <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_SR-71_Blackbird" title="Lockheed SR-71 Blackbird">SR-71</a> was soon to replace it. At the same time, new developments in reconnaissance satellite technology were nearing operation. Up to this point, the limited number of satellites available restricted coverage to the Soviet Union. A new generation of reconnaissance satellites could soon cover targets anywhere in the world. The satellites' resolution would be comparable to that of aircraft, but without the slightest political risk. Time was running out for the Tagboard.<sup id="cite_ref-Peebles_24-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peebles-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Several more test flights, including two over <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, were made from <a href="/wiki/Beale_AFB" class="mw-redirect" title="Beale AFB">Beale AFB</a>, California, in 1969 and 1970, to varying degrees of success. On 15 July 1971, Kelly Johnson received a wire canceling the D-21B program. The remaining drones were transferred by a C-5A and placed in dead storage. The tooling used to build the D-21Bs was ordered destroyed. Like the A-12 Oxcart, the D-21B Tagboard drones remained a Black airplane, even in retirement. Their existence was not suspected until August 1976, when the first group was placed in storage at the <a href="/wiki/Davis-Monthan_AFB" class="mw-redirect" title="Davis-Monthan AFB">Davis-Monthan AFB</a> <a href="/wiki/Military_Storage_and_Disposition_Center" class="mw-redirect" title="Military Storage and Disposition Center">Military Storage and Disposition Center</a>. A second group arrived in 1977. They were labeled "GTD-21Bs" (GT stood for ground training).<sup id="cite_ref-Peebles_24-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peebles-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Davis-Monthan is an open base, with public tours of the storage area at the time, so the odd-looking drones were soon spotted and photos began appearing in magazines. Speculation about the D-21Bs circulated within aviation circles for years, and it was not until 1982 that details of the Tagboard program were released. However, it was not until 1993 that the B-52/D-21B program was made public. That same year, the surviving D-21Bs were released to museums.<sup id="cite_ref-Peebles_24-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peebles-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_technology_evaluation">Foreign technology evaluation</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tonopah_Test_Range_Airport" title="Tonopah Test Range Airport">Tonopah Test Range Airport</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:4477th_Test_and_Evaluation_Squadron_MiG-21_in_flight.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/4477th_Test_and_Evaluation_Squadron_MiG-21_in_flight.jpg/220px-4477th_Test_and_Evaluation_Squadron_MiG-21_in_flight.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="118" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/4477th_Test_and_Evaluation_Squadron_MiG-21_in_flight.jpg/330px-4477th_Test_and_Evaluation_Squadron_MiG-21_in_flight.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/4477th_Test_and_Evaluation_Squadron_MiG-21_in_flight.jpg/440px-4477th_Test_and_Evaluation_Squadron_MiG-21_in_flight.jpg 2x" data-file-width="717" data-file-height="383" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:4477th_Test_and_Evaluation_Squadron_MiG-21_in_flight.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Have_Doughnut" title="Have Doughnut">HAVE DOUGHNUT</a>, a MiG-21F-13 flown by <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_Systems_Command" title="Air Force Systems Command">Air Force Systems Command</a> during its 1968 exploitation</div></div></div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, one of the missions carried out by the United States was the test and evaluation of captured <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> fighter aircraft. Beginning in the late 1960s, and for several decades, Area 51 played host to an assortment of Soviet-built aircraft. Under the <a href="/wiki/Have_Doughnut" title="Have Doughnut">HAVE DOUGHNUT</a>, <a href="/wiki/HAVE_DRILL" title="HAVE DRILL">HAVE DRILL</a> and HAVE FERRY programs, the first MiGs flown in the United States were used to evaluate the aircraft in performance, technical, and operational capabilities, pitting the types against U.S. fighters.<sup id="cite_ref-Red_Eagles_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Red_Eagles-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This was not a new mission, as testing of foreign technology by the USAF began during World War II. After the war, testing of acquired foreign technology was performed by the <a href="/wiki/Air_Technical_Intelligence_Center" class="mw-redirect" title="Air Technical Intelligence Center">Air Technical Intelligence Center</a> (ATIC, which became very influential during the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>), under the direct command of the Air Materiel Control Department. In 1961 ATIC became the Foreign Technology Division (FTD), and was reassigned to <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_Systems_Command" title="Air Force Systems Command">Air Force Systems Command</a>. ATIC personnel were sent anywhere where foreign aircraft could be found.
</p><p>The focus of <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_Systems_Command" title="Air Force Systems Command">Air Force Systems Command</a> limited the use of the fighter as a tool with which to train the <a href="/wiki/Front_line" title="Front line">front line</a> tactical fighter pilots.<sup id="cite_ref-Red_Eagles_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Red_Eagles-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Air Force Systems Command recruited its pilots from the <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_Flight_Test_Center" class="mw-redirect" title="Air Force Flight Test Center">Air Force Flight Test Center</a> at <a href="/wiki/Edwards_Air_Force_Base" title="Edwards Air Force Base">Edwards Air Force Base</a>, California, who were usually graduates from various test pilot schools. <a href="/wiki/Tactical_Air_Command" title="Tactical Air Command">Tactical Air Command</a> selected its pilots primarily from the ranks of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force_Weapons_School" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Air Force Weapons School">Weapons School</a> graduates.<sup id="cite_ref-Red_Eagles_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Red_Eagles-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 1966, <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Air_Force" title="Iraqi Air Force">Iraqi Air Force</a> fighter pilot Captain <a href="/wiki/Munir_Redfa" title="Munir Redfa">Munir Redfa</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_Cold_War_pilot_defections#Iraq" title="List of Cold War pilot defections">defected</a>, flying his <a href="/wiki/MiG-21" class="mw-redirect" title="MiG-21">MiG-21</a> to <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> after being ordered to attack Iraqi Kurd villages with napalm. His aircraft was transferred to Groom Lake in late 1967 for study. In 1968 the US Air Force and Navy jointly formed a project known as <a href="/wiki/HAVE_DOUGHNUT" class="mw-redirect" title="HAVE DOUGHNUT">HAVE DOUGHNUT</a> in which Air Force Systems Command, Tactical Air Command, and the U.S. Navy's Air Test and Evaluation Squadron Four (VX-4) flew this acquired Soviet made aircraft in simulated air combat training.<sup id="cite_ref-Red_Eagles_32-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Red_Eagles-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Because U.S. possession of the Soviet MiG-21 was, itself, secret, it was tested at Groom Lake. A joint Air Force-Navy team was assembled for a series of dogfight tests.<sup id="cite_ref-Peebles_24-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peebles-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:4477th_Test_and_Evaluation_Squadron_MiG-17F_in_flight.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/4477th_Test_and_Evaluation_Squadron_MiG-17F_in_flight.jpg/220px-4477th_Test_and_Evaluation_Squadron_MiG-17F_in_flight.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="116" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/4477th_Test_and_Evaluation_Squadron_MiG-17F_in_flight.jpg/330px-4477th_Test_and_Evaluation_Squadron_MiG-17F_in_flight.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/4477th_Test_and_Evaluation_Squadron_MiG-17F_in_flight.jpg/440px-4477th_Test_and_Evaluation_Squadron_MiG-17F_in_flight.jpg 2x" data-file-width="631" data-file-height="334" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:4477th_Test_and_Evaluation_Squadron_MiG-17F_in_flight.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>HAVE FERRY, the second of two <a href="/wiki/Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-17" title="Mikoyan-Gurevich MiG-17">MiG-17F</a> "Fresco"s loaned to the United States by <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> in 1969</div></div></div>
<p>Comparisons between the F-4 and the MiG-21 indicated that, on the surface, they were evenly matched. The HAVE DOUGHNUT tests showed the skill of the man in the cockpit was what made the difference. When the Navy or Air Force pilots flew the MiG-21, the results were a draw; the F-4 would win some fights, the MiG-21 would win others. There were no clear advantages. The problem was not with the planes, but with the pilots flying them. The pilots would not fly either plane to its limits. One of the Navy pilots was Marland W. "Doc" Townsend, then commander of <a href="/wiki/VF-121" title="VF-121">VF-121</a>, the F-4 training squadron at <a href="/wiki/NAS_Miramar" class="mw-redirect" title="NAS Miramar">NAS Miramar</a>. He was an engineer and a Korean War veteran and had flown almost every navy aircraft. When he flew against the MiG-21, he would outmaneuver it every time. The Air Force pilots would not go vertical in the MiG-21. The HAVE DOUGHNUT project officer was Tom Cassidy, a pilot with <a href="/wiki/VX-4" title="VX-4">VX-4</a>, the Navy's Air Development Squadron at <a href="/wiki/Naval_Air_Station_Point_Mugu" title="Naval Air Station Point Mugu">Point Mugu</a>. He had been watching as Townsend "waxed" the air force MiG-21 pilots. Cassidy climbed into the MiG-21 and went up against Townsend's F-4. This time the result was far different. Cassidy was willing to fight in the vertical, flying the plane to the point where it was buffeting, just above the stall. Cassidy was able to get on the F-4's tail. After the flight, they realized the MiG-21 turned better than the F-4 at lower speeds. The key was for the F-4 to keep its speed up. An F-4 had defeated the MiG-21; the weakness of the Soviet plane had been found. Further test flights confirmed what was learned. It was also clear that the MiG-21 was a formidable enemy. United States pilots would have to fly much better than they had been to beat it. This would require a special school to teach advanced air combat techniques.<sup id="cite_ref-Peebles_24-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peebles-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 12 August 1968, two Syrian air force lieutenants, Walid Adham and Radfan Rifai, took off in a pair of <a href="/wiki/MiG-17" class="mw-redirect" title="MiG-17">MiG-17</a>Fs on a training mission. They lost their way and, believing they were over Lebanon, landed at the <a href="/wiki/Betzet" title="Betzet">Betzet</a> Landing Field in northern Israel. (One version has it that they were led astray by an Arabic-speaking Israeli).<sup id="cite_ref-Peebles_24-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peebles-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Prior to the end of 1968 these MiG-17s were transferred from Israeli stocks and added to the Area 51 test fleet. The aircraft were given USAF designations and fake serial numbers so that they could be identified in DOD standard flight logs. As in the earlier program, a small group of Air Force and Navy pilots conducted mock dogfights with the MiG-17s. Selected instructors from the Navy's Top Gun school at <a href="/wiki/NAS_Miramar" class="mw-redirect" title="NAS Miramar">NAS Miramar</a>, California, were chosen to fly against the MiGs for familiarization purposes. Very soon, the MiG-17's shortcomings became clear. It had an extremely simple, even crude, control system which lacked the power-boosted controls of American aircraft. The F-4's twin engines were so powerful it could accelerate out of range of the MiG-17's guns in thirty seconds. It was important for the F-4 to keep its distance from the MiG-17. As long as the F-4 was one and a half miles from the MiG-17, it was outside the reach of the Soviet fighter's guns, but the MiG was within reach of the F-4's missiles.<sup id="cite_ref-Peebles_24-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peebles-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The data from the HAVE DOUGHNUT and HAVE DRILL tests were provided to the newly formed <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy_Fighter_Weapons_School" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Navy Fighter Weapons School">Top Gun</a> school at <a href="/wiki/NAS_Miramar" class="mw-redirect" title="NAS Miramar">NAS Miramar</a>. By 1970, the HAVE DRILL program was expanded; a few selected fleet F-4 crews were given the chance to fight the MiGs. The most important result of Project HAVE DRILL is that no Navy pilot who flew in the project defeated the MiG-17 Fresco in the first engagement. The HAVE DRILL dogfights were by invitation only. The other pilots based at Nellis Air Force Base were not to know about the U.S.-operated MiGs. To prevent any sightings, the airspace above the Groom Lake range was closed. On aeronautical maps, the exercise area was marked in red ink. The forbidden zone became known as "Red Square".<sup id="cite_ref-Peebles_24-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peebles-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the remainder of the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>, the Navy kill ratio climbed to 8.33 to 1. In contrast, the Air Force rate improved only slightly to 2.83 to 1. The reason for this difference was Top Gun. The Navy had revitalized its air combat training, while the Air Force had stayed stagnant. Most of the Navy MiG kills were by Top Gun graduates.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In May 1973, Project HAVE IDEA was formed which took over from the older HAVE DOUGHNUT, HAVE FERRY and HAVE DRILL projects and the project was transferred to the <a href="/wiki/Tonopah_Test_Range_Airport" title="Tonopah Test Range Airport">Tonopah Test Range Airport</a>. At Tonopah testing of foreign technology aircraft continued and expanded throughout the 1970s and 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-Red_Eagles_32-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Red_Eagles-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Area 51 also hosted another foreign materiel evaluation program called HAVE GLIB. This involved testing Soviet tracking and missile control radar systems. A complex of actual and replica Soviet-type threat systems began to grow around "Slater Lake", a mile northwest of the main base, along with an acquired Soviet "Barlock" search radar placed at <a href="/wiki/Tonopah_Air_Force_Station" title="Tonopah Air Force Station">Tonopah Air Force Station</a>. They were arranged to simulate a Soviet-style air defense complex.
</p><p>The Air Force began funding improvements to Area 51 in 1977 under project SCORE EVENT. In 1979, the CIA transferred jurisdiction of the Area 51 site to the Air Force Flight Test Center at Edwards AFB, California. Mr. Sam Mitchell, the last CIA commander of Area 51, relinquished command to USAF Lt. Col. Larry D. McClain.
</p>
<h3><span id="Have_Blue.2FF-117_program"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Have_Blue/F-117_program">Have Blue/F-117 program</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Have_Blue" title="Lockheed Have Blue">Lockheed Have Blue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_F-117_Nighthawk" title="Lockheed F-117 Nighthawk">Lockheed F-117 Nighthawk</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tonopah_Test_Range_Airport" title="Tonopah Test Range Airport">Tonopah Test Range Airport</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Have_Blue_bottom_view.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Have_Blue_bottom_view.jpg/220px-Have_Blue_bottom_view.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Have_Blue_bottom_view.jpg/330px-Have_Blue_bottom_view.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Have_Blue_bottom_view.jpg/440px-Have_Blue_bottom_view.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1295" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Have_Blue_bottom_view.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Underside view of <i>Have Blue</i></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Have_Blue" title="Lockheed Have Blue">Lockheed Have Blue</a> prototype stealth fighter (a smaller proof-of-concept model of the <a href="/wiki/F-117_Nighthawk" class="mw-redirect" title="F-117 Nighthawk">F-117 Nighthawk</a>) first flew at Groom in December 1977.<sup id="cite_ref-rich_haveblue_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rich_haveblue-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1978, the Air Force awarded a full-scale development contract for the F-117 to Lockheed Corporation's Advanced Development Projects. On 17 January 1981 the Lockheed test team at Area 51 accepted delivery of the first full Scale Development (FSD) prototype <i>79–780</i>, designated YF-117A. At 6:05&#160;am on 18 June 1981 Lockheed Skunk Works test pilot Hal Farley lifted the nose of YF-117A <i>79–780</i> off the runway of Area 51.<sup id="cite_ref-F117hist_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-F117hist-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/Tactical_Air_Command" title="Tactical Air Command">Tactical Air Command</a> (TAC) decided to set up a group-level organization to guide the F-117A to an initial operating capability. That organization became the 4450th Tactical Group (Initially designated "A Unit"), which officially activated on 15 October 1979 at <a href="/wiki/Nellis_AFB" class="mw-redirect" title="Nellis AFB">Nellis AFB</a>, Nevada, although the group was physically located at Area 51. The 4450th TG also operated the A-7D Corsair II as a surrogate trainer for the F-117A, and these operations continued until 15 October 1982 under the guise of an avionics test mission.<sup id="cite_ref-F117hist_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-F117hist-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Flying squadrons of the 4450th TG were the 4450th Tactical Squadron (Initially designated "I Unit") activated on 11 June 1981, and 4451st Tactical Squadron (Initially designated "P Unit") on 15 January 1983. The 4450th TS, stationed at Area 51, was the first F-117A squadron, while the 4451st TS was stationed at Nellis AFB and was equipped with <a href="/wiki/A-7D_Corsair_II" class="mw-redirect" title="A-7D Corsair II">A-7D Corsair IIs</a> painted in a dark motif, tail coded "LV". Lockheed test pilots put the YF-117 through its early paces. A-7Ds was used for pilot training before any F-117A's had been delivered by Lockheed to Area 51, later the A-7D's were used for F-117A chase testing and other weapon tests at the Nellis Range. On 15 October 1982, Major Alton C. Whitley Jr. became the first USAF 4450th TG pilot to fly the F-117A.<sup id="cite_ref-F117hist_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-F117hist-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although ideal for testing, Area 51 was not a suitable location for an operational group, so a new covert base had to be established for F-117 operations.<sup id="cite_ref-F117Area51_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-F117Area51-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tonopah_Test_Range_Airport" title="Tonopah Test Range Airport">Tonopah Test Range Airport</a> was selected for operations of the first USAF F-117 unit, the <a href="/wiki/4450th_Tactical_Group" title="4450th Tactical Group">4450th Tactical Group</a> (TG).<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> From October 1979, the Tonopah Airport base was reconstructed and expanded. The 6,000-foot runway was lengthened to 10,000 feet. Taxiways, a concrete apron, a large maintenance hangar, and a propane storage tank were added.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By early 1982, four more YF-117As were operating at the southern end of the base, known as the "Southend" or "Baja Groom Lake". After finding a large scorpion in their offices, the testing team (Designated "R Unit") adopted it as their mascot and dubbed themselves the "Baja Scorpions". Testing of a series of ultra-secret prototypes continued at Area 51 until mid-1981, when testing transitioned to the initial production of F-117 stealth fighters. The F-117s were moved to and from Area 51 by C-5 during darkness to maintain security. The aircraft were defueled, disassembled, cradled, and then loaded aboard the C-5 at night, flown to Lockheed, and unloaded at night before reassembly and flight testing. Groom performed radar profiling, F-117 weapons testing, and training of the first group of frontline USAF F-117 pilots.
</p><p>While the "Baja Scorpions" were working on the F-117, there was also another group at work in secrecy, known as "the Whalers" working on Tacit Blue. A <a href="/wiki/Fly-by-wire" title="Fly-by-wire">fly-by-wire</a> technology demonstration aircraft with curved surfaces and composite material, to evade radar, it was a prototype, and never went into production. Nevertheless, this strange-looking aircraft was responsible for many of the <a href="/wiki/Stealth_technology" title="Stealth technology">stealth technology</a> advances that were used on several other aircraft designs, and had a direct influence on the B-2; with first flight of <a href="/wiki/Tacit_Blue" class="mw-redirect" title="Tacit Blue">Tacit Blue</a> being performed on 5<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>February 1982, by <a href="/wiki/Northrop_Grumman_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Northrop Grumman Corporation">Northrop Grumman</a> test pilot, <a href="/wiki/Richard_G._Thomas" title="Richard G. Thomas">Richard G. Thomas</a>.
</p><p>Production FSD airframes from Lockheed were shipped to Area 51 for acceptance testing. As the Baja Scorpions tested the aircraft with functional check flights and L.O. verification, the operational airplanes were then transferred to the 4450th TG.<sup id="cite_ref-BScorpions_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BScorpions-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:F-117_Nighthawk_Front.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/F-117_Nighthawk_Front.jpg/220px-F-117_Nighthawk_Front.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/F-117_Nighthawk_Front.jpg/330px-F-117_Nighthawk_Front.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/F-117_Nighthawk_Front.jpg/440px-F-117_Nighthawk_Front.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2976" data-file-height="1984" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:F-117_Nighthawk_Front.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>F-117 flying over mountains</div></div></div>
<p>On 17 May 1982, the move of the 4450th TG from Groom Lake to Tonopah was initiated, with the final components of the move completed in early 1983. Production FSD airframes from Lockheed were shipped to Area 51 for acceptance testing. As the Baja Scorpions tested the aircraft with functional check flights and L.O. verification, the operational airplanes were then transferred to the 4450th TG at Tonopah.<sup id="cite_ref-BScorpions_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BScorpions-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The R-Unit was inactivated on 30 May 1989. Upon inactivation, the unit was reformed as Detachment 1, <a href="/wiki/57th_Fighter_Weapons_Wing" class="mw-redirect" title="57th Fighter Weapons Wing">57th Fighter Weapons Wing</a> (FWW). In 1990 the last F-117A (<i>843</i>) was delivered from Lockheed. After completion of acceptance flights at Area 51 of this last new F-117A aircraft, the flight test squadron continued flight test duties of refurbished aircraft after modifications by Lockheed. In February/March 1992 the test unit moved from Area 51 to the USAF Palmdale <a href="/wiki/Plant_42" class="mw-redirect" title="Plant 42">Plant 42</a> and was integrated with the <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_Systems_Command" title="Air Force Systems Command">Air Force Systems Command</a> <a href="/wiki/6510th_Test_Squadron" class="mw-redirect" title="6510th Test Squadron">6510th Test Squadron</a>. Some testing, especially RCS verification and other classified activity was still conducted at Area 51 throughout the operational lifetime of the F-117. The recently inactivated (2008) <a href="/wiki/410th_Flight_Test_Squadron" title="410th Flight Test Squadron">410th Flight Test Squadron</a> traces its roots, if not its formal lineage to the 4450th TG R-unit.<sup id="cite_ref-BScorpions_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BScorpions-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_operations">Later operations</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:F22_Area51.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/F22_Area51.jpg/220px-F22_Area51.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="125" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/F22_Area51.jpg/330px-F22_Area51.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/F22_Area51.jpg/440px-F22_Area51.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3933" data-file-height="2235" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:F22_Area51.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>F-22 during a <a href="/wiki/Red_Flag_exercise" class="mw-redirect" title="Red Flag exercise">Red Flag exercise</a> with Groom Lake in the background (March 2013)</div></div></div>
<p>Since the F-117 became operational in 1983, operations at Groom Lake have continued. The base and its associated runway system were expanded, including expansion of housing and support facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-FASOverhead_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FASOverhead-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-space_com_expand_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-space_com_expand-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In 1995, the federal government expanded the exclusionary area around the base to include nearby mountains that had hitherto afforded the only decent overlook of the base, prohibiting access to 3,972 acres (16.07&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) of land formerly administered by the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Land_Management" title="Bureau of Land Management">Bureau of Land Management</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FASOverhead_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FASOverhead-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> On 22 October 2015 a federal judge signed an order giving land that belonged to a Nevada family since the 1870s to the United States Air Force for expanding Area 51. According to the judge, the land that overlooked the base was taken to address security and safety concerns connected with their training and testing.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_status">Legal status</span></h2>
<h3><span id="U.S._government.27s_positions_on_Area_51"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._government's_positions_on_Area_51">U.S. government's positions on Area 51</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Usaf_on_area51.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Usaf_on_area51.png/170px-Usaf_on_area51.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Usaf_on_area51.png/255px-Usaf_on_area51.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Usaf_on_area51.png/340px-Usaf_on_area51.png 2x" data-file-width="688" data-file-height="900" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Usaf_on_area51.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1998 letter from the USAF replying to a query about Area 51</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CIA_BYE2369-67_page17.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/CIA_BYE2369-67_page17.gif/170px-CIA_BYE2369-67_page17.gif" decoding="async" width="170" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/CIA_BYE2369-67_page17.gif/255px-CIA_BYE2369-67_page17.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/CIA_BYE2369-67_page17.gif/340px-CIA_BYE2369-67_page17.gif 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="825" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CIA_BYE2369-67_page17.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>CIA document from 1967 referring to Area 51</div></div></div>
<p>The United States government has provided minimal information regarding Area 51. The area surrounding the lake is permanently off-limits to both civilian and normal military air traffic. Security clearances are checked regularly; cameras and weaponry are not allowed.<sup id="cite_ref-lacitis20100327_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lacitis20100327-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Even military pilots training in the <a href="/wiki/Nevada_Test_and_Training_Range" title="Nevada Test and Training Range">NAFR</a> risk disciplinary action if they stray into the exclusionary "box" surrounding Groom's airspace.<sup id="cite_ref-redflag_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-redflag-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Surveillance is supplemented using buried motion sensors.<sup id="cite_ref-poulsen_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-poulsen-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Area 51 is a common destination for <a href="/wiki/Janet_(airline)" title="Janet (airline)">Janet airline</a>, a small fleet of passenger aircraft operated on behalf of the Air Force to transport military personnel, primarily from <a href="/wiki/McCarran_International_Airport" title="McCarran International Airport">McCarran International Airport</a>.
</p><p>The USGS topographic map for the area only shows the long-disused <a href="/wiki/Groom_Mine" title="Groom Mine">Groom Mine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> A civil aviation chart published by the <a href="/wiki/Nevada_Department_of_Transportation" title="Nevada Department of Transportation">Nevada Department of Transportation</a> shows a large restricted area, defined as part of the Nellis restricted airspace.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The National Atlas shows the area as lying within the Nellis Air Force Base.<sup id="cite_ref-NationalAtlasFederalLands_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NationalAtlasFederalLands-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> There are higher resolution and newer images available from other satellite imagery providers, including Russian providers and the <a href="/wiki/IKONOS" class="mw-redirect" title="IKONOS">IKONOS</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FASOverhead_15-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FASOverhead-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> These show the runway markings, base facilities, aircraft, and vehicles.
</p><p>On 25 June 2013, the CIA released an official history of the U-2 and OXCART projects which acknowledged the existence of Area 51 in response to a Freedom of Information Act request submitted in 2005 by Jeffrey T. Richelson of <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_University" title="George Washington University">George Washington University</a>'s National Security Archive. It contains numerous references to Area 51 and Groom Lake, along with a map of the area.<sup id="cite_ref-cia1992_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia1992-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LeibyWaPo_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LeibyWaPo-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_lawsuit">Environmental lawsuit</span></h3>
<p>In 1994, five unnamed civilian contractors and the widows of contractors Walter Kasza and Robert Frost sued the Air Force and the United States Environmental Protection Agency. They alleged that they had been present when large quantities of unknown chemicals had been burned in open pits and trenches at Groom. <a href="/wiki/Rutgers_University" title="Rutgers University">Rutgers University</a> biochemists analyzed biopsies from the complainants and found high levels of <a href="/wiki/Polychlorinated_dibenzodioxins" title="Polychlorinated dibenzodioxins">dioxin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dibenzofuran" title="Dibenzofuran">dibenzofuran</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Trichloroethylene" title="Trichloroethylene">trichloroethylene</a> in their body fat. The complainants alleged that they had sustained skin, liver, and respiratory injuries due to their work at Groom, and that this had contributed to the deaths of Frost and Kasza. The suit sought compensation for the injuries, claiming that the Air Force had illegally handled toxic materials and that the EPA had failed in its duty to enforce the <a href="/wiki/Resource_Conservation_and_Recovery_Act" title="Resource Conservation and Recovery Act">Resource Conservation and Recovery Act</a> which governs handling of dangerous materials. They also sought detailed information about the chemicals, hoping that this would facilitate the medical treatment of survivors. Congressman <a href="/wiki/Lee_H._Hamilton" title="Lee H. Hamilton">Lee H. Hamilton</a>, former chairman of the House Intelligence Committee, told <i>60 Minutes</i> reporter <a href="/wiki/Lesley_Stahl" title="Lesley Stahl">Lesley Stahl</a>, "The Air Force is classifying all information about Area 51 in order to protect themselves from a lawsuit."
</p><p>The government invoked the <a href="/wiki/State_Secrets_Privilege" class="mw-redirect" title="State Secrets Privilege">State Secrets Privilege</a> and petitioned U.S. District Judge Philip Pro to disallow disclosure of classified documents or examination of secret witnesses, claiming that this would expose classified information and threaten national security.<sup id="cite_ref-lvrj_jun2002_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lvrj_jun2002-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Judge Pro rejected the government's argument, so President <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a> issued a <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Determination" title="Presidential Determination">Presidential Determination</a> exempting what it called "the Air Force's Operating Location Near Groom Lake, Nevada" from environmental disclosure laws. Consequently, Pro dismissed the suit due to lack of evidence. Turley appealed to the U.S. Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit on the grounds that the government was abusing its power to classify material. Secretary of the Air Force <a href="/wiki/Sheila_E._Widnall" class="mw-redirect" title="Sheila E. Widnall">Sheila E. Widnall</a> filed a brief which stated that disclosures of the materials present in the air and water near Groom "can reveal military operational capabilities or the nature and scope of classified operations." The Ninth Circuit rejected Turley's appeal<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> and the U.S. Supreme Court refused to hear it, putting an end to the complainants' case.
</p><p>The President annually issues a determination continuing the Groom exception<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> which is the only formal recognition that the government has ever given that Groom Lake is more than simply another part of the Nellis complex. An unclassified memo on the safe handling of <a href="/wiki/F-117_Nighthawk" class="mw-redirect" title="F-117 Nighthawk">F-117 Nighthawk</a> material was posted on an Air Force web site in 2005. This discussed the same materials for which the complainants had requested information, which the government had claimed was classified. The memo was removed shortly after journalists became aware of it.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_aviation_identification">Civil aviation identification</span></h2>
<p>In December 2007, airline pilots noticed that the base had appeared in their aircraft navigation systems' latest <a href="/wiki/Jeppesen" title="Jeppesen">Jeppesen</a> database revision with the <a href="/wiki/ICAO" class="mw-redirect" title="ICAO">ICAO</a> airport identifier code of KXTA and listed as "Homey Airport".<sup id="cite_ref-aopa-kxta-20080111_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aopa-kxta-20080111-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The probably inadvertent release of the airport data led to advice by the <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_Owners_and_Pilots_Association" title="Aircraft Owners and Pilots Association">Aircraft Owners and Pilots Association</a> (AOPA) that student pilots should be explicitly warned about KXTA, not to consider it as a waypoint or destination for any flight even though it now appears in public navigation databases.<sup id="cite_ref-aopa-kxta-20080111_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aopa-kxta-20080111-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Security">Security</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wfm_x51_area51_warningsign.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Wfm_x51_area51_warningsign.jpg/220px-Wfm_x51_area51_warningsign.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Wfm_x51_area51_warningsign.jpg/330px-Wfm_x51_area51_warningsign.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Wfm_x51_area51_warningsign.jpg/440px-Wfm_x51_area51_warningsign.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wfm_x51_area51_warningsign.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Area 51 border and warning sign stating that "photography is prohibited" and that "use of deadly force is authorized"</div></div></div>
<p>The perimeter of the base is marked out by orange posts and patrolled by guards in white pickup trucks and camouflage fatigues. The guards are popularly referred to as "cammo dudes" by enthusiasts.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nydailynews06Aug2019_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nydailynews06Aug2019-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The guards will not answer questions about their employers; however, according to the New York <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Daily_News" title="New York Daily News">Daily News</a></i>, there are indications they are employed through a contractor such as <a href="/wiki/AECOM" title="AECOM">AECOM</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nydailynews06Aug2019_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nydailynews06Aug2019-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Signage around the base perimeter advises that deadly force is authorized against <a href="/wiki/Trespass" title="Trespass">trespassers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Magazines2000_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magazines2000-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Technology is also heavily used to maintain the border of the base; this includes <a href="/wiki/Closed-circuit_television" title="Closed-circuit television">surveillance cameras</a> and motion detectors. Some of these motion detectors are placed some distance away from the base on public land to notify guards of people approaching.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1974_Skylab_photography">1974 Skylab photography</span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Dwayne_A._Day" title="Dwayne A. Day">Dwayne A. Day</a> published "Astronauts and Area 51: the <a href="/wiki/Skylab_4" title="Skylab 4">Skylab</a> Incident" in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Space_Review" title="The Space Review">The Space Review</a></i> in January 2006. It was based on a memo written in 1974 to CIA director <a href="/wiki/William_Colby" title="William Colby">William Colby</a> by an unknown CIA official. The memo reported that astronauts on board Skylab had inadvertently photographed a certain location.
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>There were specific instructions not to do this. [redacted] was the only location which had such an instruction.</p></blockquote>
<p>The name of the location was obscured,<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> but the context led Day to believe that the subject was Groom Lake. Day argues that "the CIA considered no other spot on Earth to be as sensitive as Groom Lake".<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The memo details debate between federal agencies regarding whether the images should be classified, with <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">Department of Defense</a> agencies arguing that it should and <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">State Department</a> arguing that it should not be classified. The memo itself questions the legality of retroactively classifying unclassified images.
</p><p>The memo includes handwritten remarks,<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> apparently by <a href="/wiki/Director_of_Central_Intelligence" title="Director of Central Intelligence">Director of Central Intelligence</a> Colby:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>[Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Dean_Rusk" title="Dean Rusk">Rusk</a>] did raise it—said State Dept. people felt strongly. But he inclined leave decision to me (DCI)—I confessed some question over need to protect since:
</p><ol><li><a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">USSR</a> has it from own sats</li>
<li>What really does it reveal?</li>
<li>If exposed, don't we just say classified USAF work is done there?</li></ol></blockquote>
<p>The declassified documents do not disclose the outcome of discussions regarding the Skylab imagery. The debate proved moot, as the photograph appeared in the Federal Government's Archive of Satellite Imagery along with the remaining Skylab photographs.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="UFO_and_other_conspiracy_theories">UFO and other conspiracy theories</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Groom_Lake_and_Papoose_Lake.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Groom_Lake_and_Papoose_Lake.jpg/220px-Groom_Lake_and_Papoose_Lake.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Groom_Lake_and_Papoose_Lake.jpg/330px-Groom_Lake_and_Papoose_Lake.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Groom_Lake_and_Papoose_Lake.jpg/440px-Groom_Lake_and_Papoose_Lake.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4368" data-file-height="2467" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Groom_Lake_and_Papoose_Lake.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Groom_Lake_(salt_flat)" title="Groom Lake (salt flat)">Groom Lake</a> (upper left) and <a href="/wiki/Papoose_Lake" title="Papoose Lake">Papoose Lake</a> (lower right), 2010</div></div></div>
<p>Area 51 has become a focus of modern conspiracy theories due to its secretive nature and connection to classified aircraft research. Theories include:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJacobsen2012_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJacobsen2012-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>The storage, examination, and reverse engineering of crashed alien spacecraft, including material supposedly recovered at <a href="/wiki/Roswell_UFO_incident" title="Roswell UFO incident">Roswell</a>, the study of their occupants, and the manufacture of aircraft based on alien technology</li>
<li>Meetings or joint undertakings with extraterrestrials</li>
